<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Draft genome sequence of cauliflower ( Brassica oleracea L. var. botrytis ) provides new insights into the C genome in Brassica species"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2019 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-07-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Cauliflower is an important variety of Brassica oleracea and is planted worldwide. Here, the high-quality genome sequence of cauliflower was reported. The assembled cauliflower genome was 584.60&#8201;Mb in size, with a contig N50 of 2.11&#8201;Mb, and contained 47,772 genes; 56.65% of the genome was composed of repetitive sequences. Among these sequences, long terminal repeats (LTRs) were the most abundant (32.71% of the genome), followed by transposable elements (TEs) (12.62%). Comparative genomic analysis confirmed that after an ancient paleohexaploidy (&#947;) event, cauliflower underwent two whole-genome duplication (WGD) events shared with Arabidopsis and an additional whole-genome triplication (WGT) event shared with other Brassica species. The present cultivated cauliflower diverged from the ancestral B. oleracea species ~3.0 million years ago (Mya). The speciation of cauliflower (~2.0&#8201;Mya) was later than that of B. oleracea L. var. capitata (approximately 2.6&#8201;Mya) and other Brassica species (over 2.0&#8201;Mya). Chromosome no. 03 of cauliflower shared the most syntenic blocks with the A, B, and C genomes of Brassica species and its eight other chromosomes, implying that chromosome no. 03 might be the most ancient one in the cauliflower genome, which was consistent with the chromosome being inherited from the common ancestor of Brassica species. In addition, 2,718 specific genes, 228 expanded genes, 2 contracted genes, and 1,065 positively selected genes in cauliflower were identified and functionally annotated. These findings provide new insights into the genomic diversity of Brassica species and serve as a valuable reference for molecular breeding of cauliflower."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Cauliflower is an important variety of Brassica oleracea and is planted worldwide. Here, the high-quality genome sequence of cauliflower was reported. The assembled cauliflower genome was 584.60&#8201;Mb in size, with a contig N50 of 2.11&#8201;Mb, and contained 47,772 genes; 56.65% of the genome was composed of repetitive sequences. Among these sequences, long terminal repeats (LTRs) were the most abundant (32.71% of the genome), followed by transposable elements (TEs) (12.62%). Comparative genomic analysis confirmed that after an ancient paleohexaploidy (&#947;) event, cauliflower underwent two whole-genome duplication (WGD) events shared with Arabidopsis and an additional whole-genome triplication (WGT) event shared with other Brassica species. The present cultivated cauliflower diverged from the ancestral B. oleracea species ~3.0 million years ago (Mya). The speciation of cauliflower (~2.0&#8201;Mya) was later than that of B. oleracea L. var. capitata (approximately 2.6&#8201;Mya) and other Brassica species (over 2.0&#8201;Mya). Chromosome no. 03 of cauliflower shared the most syntenic blocks with the A, B, and C genomes of Brassica species and its eight other chromosomes, implying that chromosome no. 03 might be the most ancient one in the cauliflower genome, which was consistent with the chromosome being inherited from the common ancestor of Brassica species. In addition, 2,718 specific genes, 228 expanded genes, 2 contracted genes, and 1,065 positively selected genes in cauliflower were identified and functionally annotated. These findings provide new insights into the genomic diversity of Brassica species and serve as a valuable reference for molecular breeding of cauliflower."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Cauliflower is an important variety of <i>Brassica oleracea</i> and is planted worldwide. Here, the high-quality genome sequence of cauliflower was reported. The assembled cauliflower genome was 584.60âMb in size, with a contig N50 of 2.11âMb, and contained 47,772 genes; 56.65% of the genome was composed of repetitive sequences. Among these sequences, long terminal repeats (LTRs) were the most abundant (32.71% of the genome), followed by transposable elements (TEs) (12.62%). Comparative genomic analysis confirmed that after an ancient paleohexaploidy (Î³) event, cauliflower underwent two whole-genome duplication (WGD) events shared with <i>Arabidopsis</i> and an additional whole-genome triplication (WGT) event shared with other <i>Brassica</i> species. The present cultivated cauliflower diverged from the ancestral <i>B. oleracea</i> species ~3.0 million years ago (Mya). The speciation of cauliflower (~2.0âMya) was later than that of <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i> (approximately 2.6âMya) and other <i>Brassica</i> species (over 2.0âMya). Chromosome no. 03 of cauliflower shared the most syntenic blocks with the A, B, and C genomes of <i>Brassica</i> species and its eight other chromosomes, implying that chromosome no. 03 might be the most ancient one in the cauliflower genome, which was consistent with the chromosome being inherited from the common ancestor of <i>Brassica</i> species. In addition, 2,718 specific genes, 228 expanded genes, 2 contracted genes, and 1,065 positively selected genes in cauliflower were identified and functionally annotated. These findings provide new insights into the genomic diversity of <i>Brassica</i> species and serve as a valuable reference for molecular breeding of cauliflower.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genus <i>Brassica</i> contains three basic genomes (A, B and C) that form three diploid species, namely, <i>Brassica rapa</i> (AA genome), <i>Brassica nigra</i> (BB genome), and <i>Brassica oleracea</i> (CC genome), which further hybridize to give rise to three allopolyploid species, namely, <i>Brassica napus</i> (AACC genome), <i>Brassica juncea</i> (AABB genome), and <i>Brassica carinata</i> (BBCC genome), as described by the triangle of U model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Nagaharu, U. Genome analysis in Brassica with special reference to the experimental formation of B. napus and peculiar mode of fertilication. Jpn J. Bot. 7, 389â452 (1935)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d23481e542">1</a></sup>. These <i>Brassica</i> species encompass many important vegetable and oilseed crops, such as Chinese cabbage, turnip, cabbage, broccoli, cauliflower, and oilseed rape. Among them, cauliflower (<i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>botrytis</i>, CC genome) is an important variety of <i>B. oleracea</i> that differs from most <i>Brassica</i> species in its formation of a specialized organ called the curd during floral development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Li, H. et al. Curd development associated gene (CDAG1) in cauliflower (Brassica oleracea L. var. botrytis) could result in enlarged organ size and increased biomass. Plant Sci. 254, 82â94 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d23481e562">2</a></sup>. Curds are composed of many indeterminate inflorescences and shortened inflorescence branches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Anthony, R. G., James, P. E. &amp; Jordan, B. R. The cDNA sequence of a cauliflower apetala1/squamosa homolog. Plant Physiol. 108, 441â442 (1995)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d23481e566">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Anthony, R. G., James, P. E. &amp; Jordan, B. R. Cauliflower (Brassica oleracea var. botrytis) curd development: the expression of meristem identity genes. J. Exp. Bot. 47, 181â188 (1996)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d23481e569">4</a></sup> and are the primary edible organs of cauliflower, with abundant nutrient materials, such as sulforaphane<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Cheung, K. L. &amp; Kong, A. N. Molecular targets of dietary phenethyl isothiocyanate and sulforaphane for cancer chemoprevention. AAPS J. 12, 87â97 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d23481e573">5</a></sup>. Sulforaphane is regarded as one of the most powerful natural bioactive substances in preventing and/or defending against cancers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tarozzi, A. et al. Sulforaphane as a potential protective phytochemical against neurodegenerative diseases. Oxid. Med. Cell. Longev. 2013, 415078 (2013)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d23481e577">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fisher, M. L., Ciavattone, N., Grun, D., Adhikary, G. &amp; Eckert, R. L. Sulforaphane reduces YAP/Np63alpha signaling to reduce cancer stem cell survival and tumor formation. Oncotarget 8, 73407â73418 (2017)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d23481e577_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, P. et al. Sulforaphane exerts anti-angiogenesis effects against hepatocellular carcinoma through inhibition of STAT3/HIF-1alpha/VEGF signalling. Sci. Rep. 7, 12651 (2017)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d23481e577_2">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, Q. Q. et al. Sulforaphane inhibits cancer stem-like cell properties and cisplatin resistance through miR-214-mediated downregulation of c-MYC in non-small cell lung cancer. Oncotarget 8, 12067â12080 (2017)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d23481e577_3">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Liu, C. M. et al. Sulforaphane targets cancer stemness and tumor initiating properties in oral squamous cell carcinomas via miR-200c induction. J. Formos. Med. Assoc. 116, 41â48 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d23481e580">10</a></sup>. In the past decade, the genomes of several <i>Brassica</i> species, including the <i>B. rapa</i> cultivar <i>Chiifu</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d23481e593">11</a></sup>; <i>B. oleracea</i> cultivars <i>02-12</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d23481e602">12</a></sup>, <i>TO1000DH</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Transcriptome and methylome profiling reveals relics of genome dominance in the mesopolyploid Brassica oleracea. Genome Biol. 15, R77 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d23481e609">13</a></sup>, and <i>HDEM</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Belser, C. et al. Chromosome-scale assemblies of plant genomes using nanopore long reads and optical maps. Nat. Plant 4, 879â887 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d23481e615">14</a></sup>; <i>B. nigra</i> cultivar <i>YZ12151</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yang, J. H. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet. 48, 1225â1232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d23481e624">15</a></sup>; <i>B. napus</i> cultivars Darmor<i>-bzh</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d23481e634">16</a></sup>, <i>ZS11</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Sun, F. et al. The high-quality genome of Brassica napus cultivar âZS11â reveals the introgression history in semi-winter morphotype. Plant J. 92, 452 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d23481e640">17</a></sup>, and <i>Tapidor</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bayer, P. E. et al. Assembly and comparison of two closely related Brassica napus genomes. Plant. Biotechnol. J. 15, 1602â1610 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d23481e646">18</a></sup>; and <i>B. juncea</i> cultivar <i>T84-66</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yang, J. H. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet. 48, 1225â1232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d23481e656">15</a></sup>, have been explored. These studies provided insights into the evolution and phenotypic diversification of <i>Brassica</i> species. However, our knowledge of genome variation and agriculturally important trait formation in cauliflower, one of the most important vegetable crops, is still lacking. Here, the genome of cauliflower was sequenced by PacBio and Illumina sequencing technologies to further understand the evolution of <i>Brassica</i> species, especially the genetic variation in the C genome of <i>B. oleracea</i> species, and reveal the formation of extreme morphological characteristics, namely, the enlarged inflorescences (curds).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Plant materials</h3><p>An advanced-generation inbred line of <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>botrytis</i> (C-8) was selected for whole-genome sequencing, which is widely used as a parental line for breeding due to its excellent agronomic traits. Ten-day-old seedlings of C-8 were harvested and stored at â80âÂ°C prior to DNA extraction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec4">Genome sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from the 10-day-old seedlings of C-8 by using a DNA Secure Plant Kit (TIANGEN, China) and broken into random fragments. DNA sequencing libraries were constructed in accordance with the standard Illumina library preparation protocols. Paired-end libraries with insert sizes of 350âbp were constructed according to the manufacturerâs instructions (Illumina, CA, USA). All of the constructed libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq X Ten. At least 10âÎ¼g of sheared DNA is required to construct PacBio libraries. SMRTbell template preparation involved DNA concentration, damage repair, end repair, hairpin adapter ligation, and template purification. SMRTbell libraries with an insert size of 20âkb were constructed and then sequenced on a PacBio Sequel platform (Pacific Biosciences, CA, USA) by using P6 polymerase/C4 chemistry in accordance with the manufacturerâs procedure (Pacific Biosciences, CA, USA).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec5">Estimation of genome size</h3><p>Genome size was estimated by K-mer distribution analysis. Approximately 45âGb of high-quality paired-end reads (350âbp) was generated and used to determine the abundance of 17-nt K-mers. The distribution of 17-nt K-mers depends on the characteristics of the genome and follows a Poisson distribution.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec6">Genome assembly and quality evaluation</h3><p>The de novo assembly of PacBio single-molecule long reads from Single Molecule Real Time (SMRT) sequencing was performed by using FALCON<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Pendleton, M. et al. Assembly and diploid architecture of an individual human genome via single-molecule technologies. Nat. Methods 12, 780â786 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d23481e711">19</a></sup> (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/</a>). The 60 subreads with the longest coverage were first selected as seed reads for error correction to obtain enough corrected reads. Then, the error-corrected reads were aligned to one another and assembled into genomic contigs by using FALCON with the following parameters: length_cutoff_ prâ=â5,000, max_diffâ=â120, and max_covâ=â130. The draft assembly was polished using the Quiver algorithm. Subsequently, Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d23481e722">20</a></sup> was used to perform error correction of p-contigs with the short paired-end reads generated from an Illumina HiSeq platform. The draft assembly was evaluated by mapping the high-quality reads from short-insert-size libraries to the contigs by using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. arXiv 1303, 3997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d23481e726">21</a></sup>. The distribution of the sequencing depth at each position was calculated with SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map (SAM) format and SAMtools. Transplant. Proc. 19, 1653â1654 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d23481e730">22</a></sup> to assess the completeness of the assembled genome. The GC content distribution of the assembled genome was examined to analyze the nucleotide distribution, assess the randomness of sequencing, and check for possible sample contamination. The Core Eukaryotic Genes Mapping Approach (CEGMA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d23481e735">23</a></sup> pipeline was used to assess the completeness of the genome assembly, which was further assessed by Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d23481e739">24</a></sup> analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec7">Transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>Four tissues of C-8 (leaf, curd, flower and root) were collected for RNA-seq analysis, and four sequencing libraries were constructed from these tissues using an Illumina standard mRNA-seq prep kit.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec8">Genome annotation</h3><p>TEs and other repetitive sequences in the C-8 genome were searched by combining de novo- and homology-based approaches. For the de novo-based approach, RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Smit, A. F. A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0. http://www.repeatmasker.org (2008â2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d23481e760">25</a></sup>, LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 265â268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d23481e764">26</a></sup>, and RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, 351â358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d23481e768">27</a></sup> were used to build a de novo repeat library. For the homology-based approach, the Repbase TE library and the TE protein database were searched against the cauliflower genome by utilizing RepeatMasker (version 3.3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0. http://www.repeatmasker.org (1996â2015)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d23481e772">28</a></sup> and RepeatProteinMask, respectively. Tandem repeats were detected in the genome by using Tandem Repeats Finder software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d23481e776">29</a></sup>.</p><p>Homology-based prediction and de novo-based prediction combined with transcriptome-based prediction were conducted to predict the protein-coding genes in the cauliflower genome. Homologous proteins from six plant genomes (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i>, <i>B. oleracea</i>, <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>Capsella rubella</i>, and <i>Raphanus sativus</i>) were downloaded from the Ensembl and NCBI databases. The protein sequences from these genomes were aligned to the cauliflower genome assembly by using TBLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Altschul, S., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. &amp; Lipman, D. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d23481e802">30</a></sup> with an E-value cutoff of 1e-05. BLAST hits were conjoined by Solar software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Yu, X., Zheng, H., Wang, J., Wang, W. &amp; Su, B. Detecting lineage-specific adaptive evolution of brain-expressed genes in human using rhesus macaque as outgroup. Genomics 88, 745â751 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d23481e806">31</a></sup>. GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Birney, E. &amp; Durbin, R. Using GeneWise in the Drosophila annotation experiment. Genome Res. 10, 547â548 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d23481e810">32</a></sup> was used to predict the exact gene structure of the corresponding genomic regions for each BLAST hit (Homology-set). For transcriptome-based prediction, RNA-seq data were mapped to the assembly by using TopHat (version 2.0.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d23481e814">33</a></sup>, and Cufflinks (version 2.1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511â515 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d23481e818">34</a></sup> (<a href="http://cufflinks.cbcb.umd.edu/">http://cufflinks.cbcb.umd.edu/</a>) was then used to assemble the transcripts into gene models (Cufflinks-set). In addition, the RNA-seq data were assembled by Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Trinity: reconstructing a full-length transcriptome without a genome from RNA-seq data. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644â652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d23481e830">35</a></sup> to create several pseudo-ESTs. These pseudo-ESTs were also mapped to the assembly, and gene models were predicted by PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Campbell, M. A., Hass, B. J., Hamilton, J. P., Mount, S. M. &amp; Buell, C. R. Comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in rice and comparative analyses with Arabidopsis. BMC Genom. 7, 327 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d23481e834">36</a></sup>. This gene set was denoted PASA-T-set (PASA Trinity set) and used to train <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction programs. Five <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction programs, namely, Augustus (version 2.5.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 309â312 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d23481e844">37</a></sup>, GENSCAN (version 1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Aggarwal, G. &amp; Ramaswamy, R. Ab initio gene identification: prokaryote genome annotation with GeneScan and GLIMMER. J. Biosci. 27, 7â14 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d23481e849">38</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM (version 3.0.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d23481e853">39</a></sup>, geneid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Parra, G., Blanco, E. &amp; GuigÃ³ R. GeneID in Drosophila. Genome Res. 10, 511 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d23481e857">40</a></sup>, and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Bromberg, Y. &amp; Rost, B. SNAP: predict effect of non-synonymous polymorphisms on function. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 3823 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d23481e861">41</a></sup>, were used to predict the coding regions in the repeat-masked genome. Gene model evidence from the Homology-set, Cufflinks-set, PASA-T-set and <i>ab initio</i> programs were combined by EVidenceModeler (EVM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using evidencemodeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d23481e868">42</a></sup> into a nonredundant set of gene structures.</p><p>Functional annotation of the protein-coding genes was achieved using BLASTP (E-value of 1e-05)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gish, W. &amp; States, D. J. Identification of protein coding regions by database similarity search. Nat. Genet. 3, 266â272 (1993)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d23481e875">43</a></sup> against two integrated protein sequence databases: SwissProt and NR. Protein domains were annotated by searching against the InterPro (V32.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D211âD215 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d23481e879">44</a></sup> and Pfam (V27.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Finn, R. D. et al. Pfam: the protein families database. Nat. Genet. 42, D222âD230 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d23481e883">45</a></sup> databases, using InterProScan (V4.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 116â120 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d23481e887">46</a></sup> and HMMER (V3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Finn, R. D., Clements, J. &amp; Eddy, S. R. HMMER web server: interactive sequence similarity searching. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, 29â37 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d23481e891">47</a></sup>, respectively. The Gene Ontology (GO) terms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene Ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25â29 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d23481e896">48</a></sup> for each gene were obtained from the corresponding InterPro or Pfam entry. The pathways in which the genes might be involved were assigned by BLAST against the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) database (release 53)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Kanehisa, M. et al. Data, information, knowledge and principle: back to metabolism in KEGG. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D199âD205 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d23481e900">49</a></sup>, with an E-value cutoff of 1e-05.</p><p>The tRNA genes were identified by tRNAscan-SE software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d23481e907">50</a></sup>. The rRNA fragments were predicted by aligning the rRNA sequences by using BLASTN at an E-value of 1e-10. The miRNAs and snRNAs were predicted by INFERNAL software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d23481e911">51</a></sup> against the Rfam database (release 9.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Griffithsjones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D121âD124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d23481e915">52</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec9">Gene family construction</h3><p>The protein sequences from cauliflower and other sequenced plant genomes with representatives from <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>A. lyrata</i>, <i>Aethionema arabicum</i>, <i>B. juncea</i> (AABB genome), <i>B. napus Darmor-bzh</i> (AACC genome), <i>B. napus ZS11</i> (AACC genome), <i>B. napus Tapidor</i> (AACC genome), <i>B. nigra</i> (BB genome), <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i> (CC genome), <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> (CC genome), <i>B. rapa</i> (AA genome), <i>Cardamine hirsuta</i>, <i>C. rubella</i>, <i>Eutrema salsugineum</i>, <i>Sisymbrium irio</i> and <i>Schrenkiella parvula</i> were used for gene family clustering. The gene set of each species was filtered as follows: first, when multiple transcripts were present in one gene, only the longest transcript in the coding region was obtained for further analysis; second, the genes encoding proteins of fewer than 30 amino acids were filtered out. Then, the similarity relation between the protein sequences of all of the species was obtained through BLASTP with an E-value of 1e-05. All of the protein datasets of the representative plant species were clustered into paralogs and orthologs by using the program OrthoMCL (<a href="http://orthomcl.org/orthomcl/">http://orthomcl.org/orthomcl/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d23481e988">53</a></sup> with an inflation parameter of 1.5. The three members of <i>Brassica</i> with a C genome, namely, cauliflower, <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i> and <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i>, were further clustered and subjected to Venn diagram analysis to explore the species-specific genes in cauliflower. The cauliflower-specific genes were then subjected to GO and KEGG functional annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec10">Phylogenetic tree and divergence time estimation</h3><p>The single-copy genes in the representative plant genomes were aligned by MUSCLE (<a href="http://www.drive5.com/muscle/">http://www.drive5.com/muscle/)</a><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d23481e1018">54</a></sup>, and the alignment results were combined to create a super-alignment matrix. A phylogenetic tree comprising cauliflower and other representative plant species was constructed by using RAxML (<a href="http://sco.h-its.org/exelixis/web/software/raxml/index.html">http://sco.h-its.org/exelixis/web/software/raxml/index.html</a>) with the maximum likelihood method, and the number of bootstraps was 1,000<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d23481e1029">55</a></sup>. <i>A. arabicum</i> was designated as the outgroup of the phylogenetic tree. The MCMCTree program of PAML5 (<a href="http://abacus.gene.ucl.ac.uk/software/paml.html">http://abacus.gene.ucl.ac.uk/software/paml.html</a>) was applied to infer the divergence time based on the constructed phylogenetic tree, employing the following parameters: burn-inâ=â5,000,000, sample numberâ=â1,000,000, and sample frequencyâ=â50<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d23481e1044">56</a></sup>. The calibration time of divergence of these plant species was obtained from the TimeTree database (<a href="http://www.time.org/">http://www.time.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Hedges, S. B., Dudley, J. &amp; Kumar, S. TimeTree: a public knowledge-base of divergence times among organisms. Bioinformatics 22, 2971â2972 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d23481e1055">57</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Detection of polyploidization events</h3><p>The protein sequences from cauliflower, <i>B. nigra</i>, <i>B. napus</i> Darmor<i>-bzh</i>, <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i>, and <i>B. rapa</i> were searched against themselves by using BLASTP (E-valueâ&lt;â1e-05) to identify syntenic blocks. The results were also subjected to MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486â488 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d23481e1083">58</a></sup> to determine the syntenic blocks. At least five genes were required to indicate synteny. Subsequently, the protein sequence alignments were converted into a CDS file, and 4DTv values were calculated on the basis of CDS alignments accompanying the correction of the HKY model.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Gene family expansion and contraction analysis</h3><p>The expansion and contraction of the gene families were analyzed by comparing the cluster size differences between the ancestor and each species by using the CAFÃ program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987â1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d23481e1095">59</a></sup>. A random birth and death model was used to study the changes in gene families along each lineage of the phylogenetic tree. A probabilistic graphical model (PGM) was introduced to calculate the probability of transitions in gene family size from parent to child nodes in the phylogeny. Using conditional likelihoods as the test statistics, the corresponding <i>p</i>-value in each lineage was calculated, and a <i>p</i>-value of 0.05 was used to identify the families that were significantly expanded or contracted. The expanded and contracted genes were then subjected to GO and KEGG functional annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Positively selected gene analysis</h3><p>The single-copy genes from cauliflower and two other relative species were aligned using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d23481e1114">54</a></sup> to identify the positively selected genes in cauliflower compared with other <i>Brassica</i> species with the C genome. Likelihood ratio tests based on the branch-site models of PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d23481e1121">56</a></sup> were conducted to detect positive selection, with cauliflower as a foreground branch. <i>p</i> values were computed using the Ï<sup>2</sup> statistic and corrected for multiple testing by the false discovery rate (FDR) method. The positively selected genes were subjected to GO and KEGG functional annotation.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>The cauliflower genome estimated by K-mer analysis was 603.04âMb. A total of 69.06âGb of high-quality PacBio long reads (114.52X coverage of the genome) and 45.99âGb of Illumina clean reads (76.26X coverage of the genome) were generated, resulting in approximately 190.78-fold coverage of the cauliflower genome. All of these reads were further assembled into 584.60âMb, consisting of 1,484 contigs with a contig N50 of 2.11âMb and a longest contig of 9.81âMb, which represented 96.94% of the cauliflower genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Tab1">1</a>). The high-quality reads from the short-insert paired-end libraries were mapped to the contigs by BWA-MEM to evaluate the quality of the assembled genome. Approximately 99.43% of the reads could be mapped to the assembly, which covered 99.15% of the assembled sequence (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S1</a>). The CEGMA and BUSCO pipelines were used to further assess the completeness of the genome assembly. The CEGMA results confirmed the homologs for 96.77% of the core eukaryotic genes in the assembly (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S2</a>). BUSCO analysis revealed that 97.2% of the genes in the cauliflower genome were conserved. These results verified the high quality of the presently generated cauliflower genome assembly (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S3</a>). The raw genome and transcriptome sequencing data are available from the NCBI under the project ID PRJNA516731.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics and annotated analysis of the cauliflower genome assembly</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome annotation</h3><p>The combined de novo-, homology-, and transcriptome-based predictions revealed that 108.40âMb of the 584.60-Mb assembled genome (18.54% of the genome) was annotated to code 47,772 genes, with 4.78 exons per gene on average. The average transcript length per gene was 2,035âbp, with average exon and intron lengths of 242 and 260âbp per transcript, respectively. Among the 47,772 predicted genes, 46,628 genes (97.60% of the total genes) could be annotated in at least one functional protein database. The de novo-based and homology-based approaches were also used to search for and predict repetitive sequences. A total of 56.65% of the assembled cauliflower genome was composed of repetitive sequences (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Tab1">1</a>). Among these repetitive elements, LTRs were the most abundant, accounting for 32.71% of the genome, followed by TEs (12.62%), tandem repeats (9.34%), LINEs (5.20%), simple repeats (2.89), and SINEs (0.06%) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S4</a>). In addition, 0.23% of the assembled genome was annotated as noncoding RNAs, which included 1,828 miRNAs, 1,408 tRNAs, 2,420 rRNAs, and 2,450 snRNAs (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Evolution of the cauliflower genome</h3><p>The genome sequences of the representative plant species were collected and subjected to comparative genomic analysis with cauliflower to reveal the genome evolution and divergence of cauliflower. The clustering results revealed 64,592 gene families in these plant species, and among them, 3,109 gene families were common. Furthermore, 434 of the 3,109 common gene families contained one copy in each plant species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Fig1">1a</a>). These 434 single-copy orthologous genes were used to construct the phylogenetic tree. The results confirmed that the A, B, and C genomes from different <i>Brassica</i> species were classified into three independent branches, consistent with the results described in the triangle of U model. <i>Brassica</i> species with A and C genomes displayed a shorter genetic distance than the species with the B genome. In the three <i>Brassica</i> species with the C genome, cauliflower and <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> were clustered together on a branch (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM2">S1</a>). Furthermore, the molecular clock of these plant genomes was calculated. The calibration times of divergence were 3.2â7.0 Mya between <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>, 9.1â11.3 Mya between <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>C. rubella</i>, 12.3â20.6 Mya between <i>C. rubella</i> and <i>C. hirsuta</i>, and 18.1â26.3 Mya between <i>S. parvula</i> and <i>S. irio</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Fig2">2</a>). The data further confirmed that the <i>Brassica</i> ancestor diverged from <i>S. irio</i> approximately 16.3â25.0 Mya. In <i>Brassica</i> species, the diploid species <i>B. nigra</i> with the B genome diverged from the A and C genomes approximately 9.1â13.4 Mya. Then, the other two diploid species, namely, <i>B. oleracea</i> (CC genome) and <i>B. rapa</i> (AA genome), diverged ~8.5 Mya. Furthermore, the A genome of diploid <i>B. rapa</i> diverged into the A subgenome of allotetraploid <i>B. juncea</i> 2.3â4.1 Mya and <i>B. napus</i> 2.8â4.8 Mya. The three subgenomes of <i>B. napus</i> diverged 2.4â4.0 Mya. The C genome of the <i>B. oleracea</i> ancestral species diverged into the C subgenome of allotetraploid <i>B. napus</i>, uncultivated <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> (wild cabbage) and two varieties, specifically, cauliflower and <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i>, approximately 3.0 Mya. <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> diverged from the variety <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i> approximately 2.6 Mya, whereas the divergence time between cauliflower and <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> was ~2.0 Mya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Fig2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" <i>Brassica</i> species with the C genome</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The nodes represent the divergence time from present (million years ago, Mya). The blue number in the brackets indicates the confidence interval of the divergence time. <b>aâc</b> indicate the <b>A</b>, <b>B</b> and <b>C</b> genomes of <i>Brassica</i> species, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Synteny analysis was conducted in cauliflower and four other representative <i>Brassica</i> species, namely, <i>B. nigra</i>, <i>B. napus</i> Darmor<i>-bzh</i>, <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i>, and <i>B. rapa</i>, to further understand the genome evolution of cauliflower. The results indicated that 607 syntenic blocks, which involved 31,380 genes, were present between cauliflower and <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Fig3">3</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S7</a>). Among the 607 syntenic blocks, 103 were distributed on chromosome no. 03 of cauliflower, representing the most syntenic blocks on the 9 chromosomes, followed by 79 on chromosome no. 07; 73 on chromosome no. 04; 71 on chromosome no. 08; 61 on chromosome no. 09; 59 on chromosome no. 02; 58 on chromosome no. 06; 55 on chromosome no. 05; and 48 on chromosome no. 01. A total of 777 syntenic blocks were detected between cauliflower and <i>B. nigra</i>, which were composed of 34,361 genes (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Fig3">3</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S9</a>). Similarly, chromosome no. 03 of cauliflower shared the most syntenic blocks (122) with the eight chromosomes of <i>B. nigra</i>, followed by chromosome nos. 04 and 07. A total of 761 syntenic blocks existed between cauliflower and <i>B. rapa</i>. Among them, 126 were distributed on chromosome no. 03 of cauliflower, also representing the most syntenic blocks on the 9 chromosomes. These combined 761 syntenic blocks were composed of 39,449 genes (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Fig3">3</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S11</a>). These results indicated that chromosome no. 03 of cauliflower shared the most syntenic blocks with the A and B genomes of <i>Brassica</i> species and the C genome of <i>B. oleracea</i>. Moreover, synteny analysis of the 9 chromosomes of cauliflower confirmed that chromosome no. 03 shared the most syntenic blocks with the 8 other chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Fig4">4</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S1</a>2 and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S13</a>). These findings suggested that chromosome no. 03 is the most ancient one in the cauliflower genome, which was inherited from the common ancestor of <i>Brassica</i> species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Synteny analysis of genes in cauliflower, B. nigra, B. rapa and B. oleracea L. var. capitata."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Synteny analysis of genes in cauliflower, <i>B. nigra</i>, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0164-0/MediaObjects/41438_2019_164_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0164-0/MediaObjects/41438_2019_164_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="338" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Syntenic blocks of cauliflower with <i>B. nigra</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>. <b>b</b> Syntenic blocks of cauliflower with <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i>. The numbers indicate the corresponding chromosomes in each species. The detailed syntenic blocks and the associated genes are shown in Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S6</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S13</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Circos diagram of different elements on the chromosomes of cauliflower."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Circos diagram of different elements on the chromosomes of cauliflower.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0164-0/MediaObjects/41438_2019_164_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0164-0/MediaObjects/41438_2019_164_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="734" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Chrom. 01, 02, 03, 04, 05, 06, 07, 08, and 09 indicate the nine assembled chromosomes of cauliflower</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>According to the syntenic blocks detected in cauliflower and four other <i>Brassica</i> species, 4DTv values were estimated. The data revealed three peaks at approximately 0.15, 0.33, and 0.6 in cauliflower and four other <i>Brassica</i> species, whereas only two peaks at approximately 0.6 and 0.33 were detected in <i>Arabidopsis</i>. The peaks at approximately 0.6 and 0.33 revealed two WGD events (Î² and then Î±) shared with <i>Arabidopsis</i> after the ancient Î³ event in cauliflower and other <i>Brassica</i> species. The other peak at ~0.15 indicated an additional WGT event shared among <i>Brassica</i> species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Fig5">5</a>). Moreover, the 4DTv analysis confirmed that the cauliflower genome first diverged from that of <i>B. nigra</i> (BB genome) and then diverged from that of <i>B. rapa</i> (AA genome). The genomes of cauliflower and <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> and the C subgenome of <i>B. napus</i> Darmor<i>-bzh</i> arose recently and did not exhibit significant divergence compared with the A and B genomes of <i>Brassica</i> species. This finding was consistent with the phylogenetic analysis of <i>Brassica</i> and other representative <i>Cruciferae</i> species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Fig5">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Distribution of 4DTv distances."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Distribution of 4DTv distances.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0164-0/MediaObjects/41438_2019_164_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-019-0164-0/MediaObjects/41438_2019_164_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="576" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The <i>x</i> axis indicates the 4Dtv distance. The <i>y</i> axis indicates the percentage of gene pairs. (C) shows the C subgenome of <i>B. napus Darmor-bzh</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Specific gene families in cauliflower</h3><p>Comparative genomic analysis was conducted to determine the specific genes only present in cauliflower. In three <i>Brassica</i> species with the C genome, namely, cauliflower, <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i> and <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i>, 20,223 gene families were common. Compared with those in <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i> and <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i>, 2,116 gene families, including 2,718 genes, were cauliflower specific (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#Fig1">1b</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S14</a>). Functional annotation results confirmed that these genes were significantly enriched in the GO terms involved in respiratory electron transport chain, ribosome, translation, ribonucleoprotein complex, structural constituent of ribosome, RNAâDNA hybrid ribonuclease activity, tryptophanyl-tRNA aminoacylation, intracellular organelle, structural molecule activity, endopeptidase inhibitor activity, and tryptophan-tRNA ligase activity (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S15</a>). KEGG analysis indicated that these specific genes were mainly involved in metabolic pathways such as mismatch repair, DNA replication, homologous recombination, nucleotide excision repair, oxidative phosphorylation, beta-alanine metabolism, benzoxazinoid biosynthesis and biosynthesis of siderophore-group nonribosomal peptides (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S16</a>). These data indicated that the cauliflower-specific gene families were mainly involved in the biosynthesis, metabolism, and modification of proteins and nucleic acids.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Expansion and contraction of gene families in cauliflower</h3><p>The expansion and contraction of the gene families in cauliflower were explored by comparing them with the gene families in <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i> and <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i>. The results indicated that 6 gene families, including 228 genes, were significantly expanded in cauliflower, and these genes were mainly involved in peptidyl-prolyl <i>cis-trans</i> isomerase, armadillo repeat-containing kinesin, and senescence-associated protein (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S17</a>). Functional categories indicated that these expanded genes were significantly enriched in protein folding and zinc ion binding. In contrast, only 2 genes significantly contracted in cauliflower: UDP-glycosyltransferase and elongation factor 1-alpha 4. These contracted genes were significantly enriched in zeatin biosynthesis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Positively selected genes in cauliflower</h3><p>The Ka/Ks of the single-copy genes was evaluated by using cauliflower as a foreground branch and two other related <i>Brassica</i> species (<i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i> and <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i>) as a background branch to identify the positively selected genes in cauliflower. A total of 1,065 candidate genes in cauliflower underwent positive selection (<i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S18</a>). Most of them were enriched in the GO terms involved in nucleic acid binding, cellular nitrogen compound metabolism, organic cyclic compound metabolism, heterocycle metabolism, and nucleobase-containing compound metabolism (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#MOESM1">S19</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec21" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec21-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec21">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec21-content"><p>Cauliflower is a variety of <i>B. oleracea</i> and an important vegetable crop. <i>B. oleracea</i> also includes other vegetable crops, such as cabbage, broccoli, kale, brussels sprouts, collard greens, savoy, kohlrabi, and gai lan. These varieties display very high phenotypic diversity, even though they originated from the same ancestor. Cauliflower and broccoli exhibit extremely enlarged inflorescence meristems and/or flower buds, which form the major edible organ called the curd. Cabbage and brussels sprouts display extremely enlarged terminal leaf buds and lateral leaf buds, respectively. The leaves of kale and collard greens are obviously different from those of uncultivated wild cabbage, whereas kohlrabi has a dramatically enlarged stem, which is its edible organ. With the rapid development of high-throughput sequencing technologies, whole-genome sequencing can now be conducted to reveal the phenotypic diversity and evolution of nonmodel plant species. In <i>Brassica</i> species, the genomes of at least nine species or varieties, including one cultivar from <i>B. rapa</i> with the AA genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2107">11</a></sup>, one cultivar from <i>B. nigra</i> with the BB genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yang, J. H. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet. 48, 1225â1232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2115">15</a></sup>, three cultivars from <i>B. oleracea</i> with the CC genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. In the present study, the genome of another important cultivar from <i>B. oleracea</i>, cauliflower, was uncovered. A 584.60-Mb cauliflower genome assembly with 47,772 genes was reported. The repetitive sequences comprised 56.65% of the genome. The cauliflower genome was larger than the A genome of <i>B. rapa</i> (238.32âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al.
