Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2208">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun, F. et al. The high-quality genome of Brassica napus cultivar âZS11â reveals the introgression history in semi-winter morphotype. Plant J. 92, 452 (2017)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2208_1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bayer, P. E. et al. Assembly and comparison of two closely related Brassica napus genomes. Plant. Biotechnol. J. 15, 1602â1610 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2211">18</a></sup>. However, the number of genes in cauliflower was similar to or less than that in <i>Brassica</i> species with the C genome. These findings indicated that a high-quality cauliflower genome was generated and suggested that repetitive sequences and other noncoding sequences were more abundant in cauliflower than in other <i>B. oleracea</i> species. The present genomic data provide valuable references for understanding the formation and regulation of important agronomic traits in cauliflower and related species.</p><p>Cauliflower was domesticated from wild <i>B. oleracea</i>, which originally grew in the coastal Mediterranean. According to historical records, cauliflower was first introduced to France from Genoa, Italy, in the 16th century, but it did not commonly appear on grand tables until the time of Louis XIV. In the 18th century, cauliflower was introduced to India, and it was introduced to southern China in the middle of the 19th century. Currently, China is one of the countries that cultivates and consumes the most cauliflower. However, the origin and evolution of cauliflower in the genus <i>Brassica</i> is still ambiguous. Nevertheless, genomic data from other <i>Brassica</i> species have provided significant insight into the evolutionary relationships of plant species in the genus <i>Brassica</i> as well as in the whole <i>Cruciferae</i>. The <i>Brassica</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i> lineages diverged approximately 20â35 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yang, Y. W., Lai, K. N., Tai, P. Y. &amp; Li, W. H. Rates of nucleotide substitution in angiosperm mitochondrial DNA sequences and dates of divergence between Brassica and other angiosperm lineages. J. Mol. Evol. 48, 597â604 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2246">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Town, C. D. et al. Comparative genomics of Brassica oleracea and Arabidopsis thaliana reveal gene loss, fragmentation, and dispersal after polyploidy. Plant Cell 18, 1348â1359 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2249">61</a></sup>. <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> diverged from the <i>A. thaliana</i> lineage 5â9<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2263">11</a></sup> and 4.6 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2267">12</a></sup>, respectively. Good estimates indicated the separation of <i>B. oleracea</i> from <i>B. rapa</i> approximately 4 Mya, and <i>B. nigra</i> separated from the A/C lineage 6â14.6 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Navabi, Z. K. et al. Conserved microstructure of the Brassica B Genome of Brassica nigra inrelation to homologous regions of Arabidopsis thaliana, B. rapa and B. oleracea. BMC Genom. 14, 250 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2280">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Lysak, M. A., Koch, M. A., Pecinka, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Chromosome triplication found across the tribe Brassiceae. Genome Res. 15, 516â525 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2283">63</a></sup>. The divergence time between <i>B. napus</i> and its progenitors was ~7500 years ago or less, based on different methods of estimation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2291">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Sun, F. et al. The high-quality genome of Brassica napus cultivar âZS11â reveals the introgression history in semi-winter morphotype. Plant J. 92, 452 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2294">17</a></sup>. Consistent with previous investigations, the present cauliflower genomic data and phylogenetic analysis confirmed that the <i>Brassica</i> species with the B genome, such as <i>B. nigra</i>, are more ancient than those of <i>Brassica</i> species with the A or C genome. The <i>Brassica</i> species with the A genome and those with the B genome diverged approximately 8.5 Mya. The <i>Brassica</i> species with the A genome further diverged ~3.8 Mya, whereas those with the C genome diverged ~3.0 Mya. These results implied that the <i>Brassica</i> species with the A genome appeared earlier than those with the C genome. The C genome of the <i>B. oleracea</i> ancestral species diverged into the C subgenome of allotetraploid <i>B. napus</i> and the C genome of diploid varieties, including <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i>, <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i>, and cauliflower, implying that the C subgenome in <i>B. napus</i> originated from the genome of the ancestral diploid <i>B. oleracea</i> species, while it was different from the genome of the modern diploid <i>B. oleracea</i> species. The present results also confirmed that <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i> was generated earlier than cauliflower. The cauliflower genome was likely generated ~2.0 Mya, making cauliflower the youngest known variety of <i>B. oleracea</i> as well as in the genus <i>Brassica</i>.</p><p>Except for the specialized organ, the curd, the phenotypes of cauliflower are similar to those of closely related species in the genus <i>Brassica</i>, such as uncultivated <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i>. Especially in the seedling phase, distinguishing one species from another is difficult. Consequently, identifying the differential genes between cauliflower and closely related species is important for uncovering the phenotypic specificity of cauliflower. Here, comparative genomic analysis of cauliflower and two other <i>B. oleracea</i> species, namely, <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i>, confirmed that 2,116 gene families were cauliflower specific, while only six gene families and two gene families were significantly expanded and contracted in cauliflower, respectively. In addition, compared with the genes in <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i>, 1,065 positively selected genes were identified in cauliflower. These specific, positively selected, expanded, and contracted genes provided valuable insight into the formation of phenotypic characteristics and evolution of cauliflower. For example, several GO terms or metabolic pathways targeted by the cauliflower-specific gene families displayed significant enrichment in tryptophanyl-tRNA aminoacylation and tryptophan-tRNA ligase activity. Tryptophan is an important precursor of auxin biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Bartel, B. Auxin biosynthesis. Ann. Rev. Plant Physiol. Mol. Biol. 48, 51â66 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2393">64</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Kasahara, H. Current aspects of auxin biosynthesis in plants. Biosci. Biotechnol. Biochem. 80, 34â42 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2396">65</a></sup>. The only two contracted gene families compared with <i>B. oleracea TO1000DH</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> L. var. <i>capitata</i> are both involved in zeatin biosynthesis, which plays crucial roles in cytokinin biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Astot, C. et al. An alternative cytokinin biosynthesis pathway. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 14778â14783 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-019-0164-0#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d23481e2409">66</a>,67]</sup>. These results indicated that novel genes involved in auxin biosynthesis might have been generated during the evolution of cauliflower. However, the genes involved in cytokinin biosynthesis were contracted. Auxin and cytokinin are two important phytohormones that mainly function in regulating plant growth and development. The adjustment of the homeostasis of auxin and cytokinin in cauliflower relative to that in other <i>B. oleracea</i> species might be associated with the phenotypic characteristics of cauliflower.</p><p>In conclusion, the high-quality cauliflower genome was reported. Cauliflower underwent two WGD events and one WGT event after the ancient Î³ event. Chromosome no. 03 may be the most ancient one in the cauliflower genome, as it was inherited from the common ancestor of <i>Brassica</i> species. Cauliflower may be the youngest known variety in the genus <i>Brassica</i>. Moreover, specific, positively selected, expanded and contracted genes that may be closely associated with the phenotypic characteristics and evolution of cauliflower were identified. These findings provide new insight into the genomic diversity of <i>Brassica</i> species and are valuable for molecular breeding of cauliflower.</p></div></div></section>
